    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_15572171">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15572171" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/381149-martine" class="left imgcol" title="Martine"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1189863081p2/381149.jpg" alt="Martine" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/15572171?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 27, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/381149-martine" class="user" title="Martine">Martine</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/461121.The_Hours" title="Paperback 9780312243029" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText"> those who love an intense look at life</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=psychological-drama" class="actionLinkLite">psychological-drama</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=modern-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">modern-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=film" class="actionLinkLite">film</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=north-american" class="actionLinkLite">north-american</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=glbt" class="actionLinkLite">glbt</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer15572171">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10466710156701450260">Several years ago I had the fortune of watching the film adaptation of <em>The Hours</em>, which quite blew me away. I'm not sure why it then took me so long to read the book on which the film was based, but I'm glad I did, as it's just beautiful. <br /><br /><em>The Hours</em> is both a tribute to and an update of Virginia Woolf's 1920s classic <em>Mrs Dalloway</em>, in which Pulitzer-winning author Michael Cunningham tries to answer the question of how Woolf's characters would interact in a present-day setting. Short on action but</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10466710156701450260">Several years ago I had the fortune of watching the film adaptation of <em>The Hours</em>, which quite blew me away. I'm not sure why it then took me so long to read the book on which the film was based, but I'm glad I did, as it's just beautiful. <br /><br /><em>The Hours</em> is both a tribute to and an update of Virginia Woolf's 1920s classic <em>Mrs Dalloway</em>, in which Pulitzer-winning author Michael Cunningham tries to answer the question of how Woolf's characters would interact in a present-day setting. Short on action but long on memories, associations and momentous decisions, it's a character study of three women who are looking for some meaning in their lives. First of all, there's Woolf herself, recovering from a bout of mental illness and busy outlining the novel that will eventually become <em>Mrs Dalloway</em>. Secondly, there's Laura Brown, a 1940s American housewife who wishes to lose herself in the experience of reading <em>Mrs Dalloway</em>. And thirdly, there's Clarissa Vaughan, a modern fifty-something New Yorker who is nicknamed Mrs Dalloway and who experiences a day not unlike the one Woolf describes in <em>Mrs Dalloway</em>. Although the three women seem very different on the outset, they do in fact have a fair bit in common. For one thing, they're all perfectionists who obsess about little details and continually fall short of their own standards and expectations. For another, they all, for various reasons, feel trapped and want OUT. And finally, they're all prone to obsessing about the past -- to wondering why they made the decisions they made, and whether their lives would have been very different if they had made different choices at the time. Cunningham first lets his women dwell on those questions for a while, then has them either accept their current lives or find a way out. Their internal drama and eventual decisions make for a brilliant meditation on past and present, on choices and on resignation to those choices. Among other things, the book tells you what it's like to realise that you may have had your moment and let it slip through your fingers, never to regain it. It shows you what it's like to be a dreadful perfectionist, doomed always to let yourself down. It shows you love, life, death. Especially the latter. More than any other other novel I've read, <em>The Hours</em> is steeped in death. That probably sounds depressing, but it's really quite beautiful, if you're morbid enough to see the beauty in that sort of thing. Especially since it is in its own way quite life-affirming.<br /><br />I could say a lot about the fabulous way in which Cunningham links his three stories -- about the many recurring themes, associations and echoes which make this such a hauntingly beautiful book. I could also say a lot about the respectful (and in my opinion successful) way in which he elaborates on the themes and questions raised in <em>Mrs Dalloway</em> (a book you needn't have read in order to appreciate <em>The Hours</em>, although it will definitely improve your understanding and enjoyment of the latter if you have). However, the main achievement of the book, as far as I'm concerned, is the marvellous way in which Cunningham paints emotions, more specifically emotions associated with depression, angst, melancholy and regret. I think you need to have been depressed or bipolar yourself really to understand the violent mood swings through which Cunningham puts his protagonists -- rapid transitions from despair to epiphany, from frustration to gratitude and exultation, from spiritual numbness to lyrical rapture, from ferocious neuroticism to a calm resignation to things. Having been there myself, I could relate only too well. I'm sure the same is true for many people -- men as well as women.<br /><br />I'm taking one star off because Cunningham's shifts in perspective can be a tad jarring and because the dialogue is occasionally monotonous (all Cunningham's characters sound like the author himself, which is not a big deal as he's very articulate, but is still a bit of a flaw). Apart from that, though, <em>The Hours</em> is a great book which I highly recommend to anyone -- not just to neurotic women. :-)</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10466710156701450260">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <a href="/book/show/461121.The_Hours" title="Paperback 9780312243029" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=rth-lifetime" class="actionLinkLite">rth-lifetime</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1360886265">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9005605813448364230">Here's what heterosexual sex is like:<blockquote>She thinks sometimes, can't help thinking, of those cans of peanuts sold in novelty shops, the ones with the paper snakes waiting to pop out when the lids are opened. There will be no reading tonight.</blockquote>There will be no heterosexual sex in this novel, in which all the main characters will at least consider suicide and also make out with other girls. At one point Virginia Woolf makes out with her sister, which I'm not sure that really happened. <br /><br />There are three th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9005605813448364230">Here's what heterosexual sex is like:<blockquote>She thinks sometimes, can't help thinking, of those cans of peanuts sold in novelty shops, the ones with the paper snakes waiting to pop out when the lids are opened. There will be no reading tonight.</blockquote>There will be no heterosexual sex in this novel, in which all the main characters will at least consider suicide and also make out with other girls. At one point Virginia Woolf makes out with her sister, which I'm not sure that really happened. <br /><br />There are three thematically linked stories here, each spanning a single day. The first stars Virginia Woolf herself as she begins one of her best books, <a title="My review" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1020784583">Mrs. Dalloway.</a> The second focuses on a woman named Clarissa in 2001 as she plans her own party, very loosely mirroring the plot of Mrs. Dalloway. (Her friend Richard, veteran of a different kind of war, sits in for Septimus.) And the third, smallest and in some way scariest one stars a pregnant Mrs. Brown in the 50s as she reads Mrs. Dalloway in a shitty hotel room. <br /><br />It's a wonderful book: perfectly planned and executed, exceptionally written, engaging to read and very moving. Cunningham has totally knocked this out of the park, and you can see why it won a Pulitzer. It joins <a title="My review" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/207519267">The Master</a> on my list of weird brilliant semi-fictionalized metafictional books starring other authors. <br /><br />You should read <em>Mrs. Dalloway</em> before this. <em>The Hours</em> contains major spoilers for <em>Mrs. Dalloway,</em> and anyway you should just read <em>Dalloway</em> in general, it's fuckin' awesome.</span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <a href="/user/show/1713956-manny" class="user" title="Manny">Manny</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer39430953">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14150302638465544326">First I saw the movie; then I read <em>Mrs Dalloway</em>; and finally I read the book. With this unusual order, I really liked it. But given that a lot of other reviewers seem dismissive, it's possible that Cunningham is getting a free ride from Virginia Woolf, Julianne Moore, Meryl Streep and Nicole Kidman. <br /><br />Whatever the truth of the matter, and despite the fact that I did it more or less by accident, I recommend the recipe!<br /><br /></span>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41029569" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1630617-moira-russell" class="left imgcol" title="Moira Russell"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1256449807p2/1630617.jpg" alt="Moira Russell" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/41029569?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 27, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1630617-moira-russell" class="user" title="Moira Russell">Moira Russell</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/598662.The_Hours" title="Hardcover 9780374172893" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1630617-moira-russell?shelf=underwhelmed" class="actionLinkLite">underwhelmed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer41029569">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8101510151017920854">I've read this book at least twice, judging from dog-ears and annotations, and still have no real clear memory of it. And I don't think my memory's _that_ bad. Cunningham brings new meaning to the words "riding on coat-tails," as well as unhappily eliding one of Woolf's most joyously creative periods -- her composition of the novel he pallidly imitates -- with the dark helplessness just before her suicide. Cunningham winds up rather nastily perpetuating the myth of the Frail Suicidal Lady Genius</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8101510151017920854">I've read this book at least twice, judging from dog-ears and annotations, and still have no real clear memory of it. And I don't think my memory's _that_ bad. Cunningham brings new meaning to the words "riding on coat-tails," as well as unhappily eliding one of Woolf's most joyously creative periods -- her composition of the novel he pallidly imitates -- with the dark helplessness just before her suicide. Cunningham winds up rather nastily perpetuating the myth of the Frail Suicidal Lady Genius while simultaneously reaping the rewards of her real work. Extra fail points for having the modern Septimus Warren Smith character commit suicide because his mother is a Bad Mummy, rather than Woolf's incisive psychological commentary on shellshock and WWI.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8101510151017920854">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a href="/user/show/2499261-rachel" style="font-weight:bold">rachel</a>

      
<span id="freeTextContainer12661637961413734638">"Frail Suicidal Lady Genius" is one of my least favorite literary tropes, not because women like V. Woolf and Sylvia Plath don't fascinate me in reali</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12661637961413734638">"Frail Suicidal Lady Genius" is one of my least favorite literary tropes, not because women like V. Woolf and Sylvia Plath don't fascinate me in reality, but because there's a big risk that they'll be rendered in this falsely noble and well, <em>frail</em> sort of way that beautifies depression and/or reinforces stereotypes about depressed women. Cunningham's prose in this one really bothered me, for that reason.<br /><br />I mean, have you ever read a Plath poem? Have you ever heard her read one of her poems? (General you, not you Moira.) There's a fury there that's anything but frail.</span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6948920250114153698">Richard does not kill himself over his mother; he kills himself because he can't take what AIDS and pain have done to his mind. I don't see how this is any different (substantively) from Septimus' shellshock. I did not read Virginia as frail; her suicide is tied to her pain. We are, after all, given a time for her death that reveals that it is separated from the time she wrote <em>Mrs. Dalloway</em>; I thought she was portrayed as strong and struggling, desperately trying to love the world around her despite everything. I found this book to contain vast hope and to portray Virginia's suicide in a sympathetic yet not "glorious" light.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer4841407">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer252286944576010878">When I first read this book, I loved it. But as I spent time thinking about it, I found myself liking the novel less and less. Finally I went back and re-read it, and on the second reading I truly disliked it. Partly, things that had seemed profound or beautiful looked much more flimsy on second glance...even trite, sometimes. Like a facade of depth, if that makes any sense. Which is not to say that Cunningham isn't completely sincere and genuine in his approach to his subject. Just that I'm not</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText252286944576010878">When I first read this book, I loved it. But as I spent time thinking about it, I found myself liking the novel less and less. Finally I went back and re-read it, and on the second reading I truly disliked it. Partly, things that had seemed profound or beautiful looked much more flimsy on second glance...even trite, sometimes. Like a facade of depth, if that makes any sense. Which is not to say that Cunningham isn't completely sincere and genuine in his approach to his subject. Just that I'm not sure he went as far as the aura of the novel might suggest. Also, Mrs. Dalloway is a favorite of mine, and the more time I spent with the Hours, the less I agreed with its take on that book, or its author.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_530707448">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/530707448" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/442654-andrew" class="left imgcol" title="Andrew"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1228898290p2/442654.jpg" alt="Andrew" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/530707448?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 14, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/442654-andrew" class="user" title="Andrew">Andrew</a>

          added it




        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=american-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">american-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=90s-american-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">90s-american-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer530707448">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1561836166273935342">Oh, hello there! I'm Michael Cunningham.<br /><br />It turns out that if you simply name-check and extensively quote a legitimate masterwork of English literature-- and one written by a woman in the 1920's, who is therefore oppressed and far more sympathetic to the modern reader (and, more importantly to my eyes, critic for a major current-affairs magazine) than, say, T.S. Eliot or James Joyce-- people will think you're a genius too!<br /><br />It's really quite simple. If you call your acts of craven near-plagiarism "</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1561836166273935342">Oh, hello there! I'm Michael Cunningham.<br /><br />It turns out that if you simply name-check and extensively quote a legitimate masterwork of English literature-- and one written by a woman in the 1920's, who is therefore oppressed and far more sympathetic to the modern reader (and, more importantly to my eyes, critic for a major current-affairs magazine) than, say, T.S. Eliot or James Joyce-- people will think you're a genius too!<br /><br />It's really quite simple. If you call your acts of craven near-plagiarism "postmodernity" without ever really asking what postmodernity means, why people use it as a literary technique, or any of the aesthetic or epistemological considerations embodied therein, then it turns out that you've written a book with "multiple levels" and (if you're getting fancy!) "intertextuality." Which is like Inception! And that movie, made, like, 50 trillion dollars or something and had a bunch of sexy famous people in it!<br /><br />(Yes, I know Inception came out more than a decade after The Hours was written. Like I give a fuck. &lt;3, Actual Andrew)<br /><br />Oh, they've all got plenty of funds to do more or less what they want. But one of them complains that she's not "New York rich." What a DRAG that is!<br /><br />Hey, all the people in my book The Hours have sex with people of the SAME sex! That makes this EDGY! Don't let Grandma find out!<br /><br />Oh, and this got made into a movie! It even got nominated for Oscars and things! Lots of people liked this because it had close-ups of pale and quivering white-woman hands and long shots of wistful autumnal landscapes. Turns out that, thanks to my intrinsic genius which I've already established, any movie that gets made out of my books becomes an "art film," which means lots of people in cardigans will go see it.<br /><br />(Actual Andrew again. I didn't hate this book at the face of it. Michael Cunningham can write a sentence, but he fits them all into a work of stunning mediocrity, and do it all while piggybacking on one of my favorite English language novels. This is the book I would write if someone told me to write something while indulging every one of my bad writing habits. What pisses me off so much is that the author was actually had enough chutzpah and enough lack of literary ambition to do it.)</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1561836166273935342">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/86145-stephanie?shelf=contemporaryfiction" class="actionLinkLite">contemporaryfiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer2036111">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7633039068140343050">i kind of wonder what would have happened if this hadn't been my first cunningham. i didn't like any of his other books anywhere near as much (i full on hated <a title=" Specimen Days" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20Specimen%20Days"> Specimen Days</a>). but this, somehow, i think it has to do with the way he manages to write virginia in a way that seems pretty true, that won me over. also, the modern-day "mrs. dalloway" breaks my heart every time i read the damn thing. <br /><br />i think i liked it so much because it was so ambitious, and i thought he pulled it off. i mean, to write</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7633039068140343050">i kind of wonder what would have happened if this hadn't been my first cunningham. i didn't like any of his other books anywhere near as much (i full on hated <a title=" Specimen Days" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20Specimen%20Days"> Specimen Days</a>). but this, somehow, i think it has to do with the way he manages to write virginia in a way that seems pretty true, that won me over. also, the modern-day "mrs. dalloway" breaks my heart every time i read the damn thing. <br /><br />i think i liked it so much because it was so ambitious, and i thought he pulled it off. i mean, to write virginia woolf as she's writing <em>mrs. dalloway</em>, a woman in the 50s reading it, and a woman in modern day kind of living it - it's so . . . not what you would expect. i don't know. i thought it was brilliant at the time. i was sad they made it into a movie, but it turned out better than i thought, so. but really, this is his only book that i would recommend. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_542107506">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/542107506" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/10382635-ginny-1807" class="left imgcol" title="Ginny_1807"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1404130918p2/10382635.jpg" alt="Ginny_1807" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/542107506?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 01, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/10382635-ginny-1807" class="user" title="Ginny_1807">Ginny_1807</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/393197.Le_ore" title="Paperback 9788845249778" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Ginny_1807 by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/9777043-s-ap">S©aP</a>
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/10382635-ginny-1807?shelf=romanzi" class="actionLinkLite">romanzi</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10382635-ginny-1807?shelf=dal-film-al-libro-e-viceversa" class="actionLinkLite">dal-film-al-libro-e-viceversa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer542107506">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15181698944642681723">Un'opera letteraria può essere pensata, scritta, letta, amata e addirittura vissuta. <br />E può divenire, travalicando i confini dello spazio e del tempo, una sorta di collegamento tra persone molto dissimili tra loro, eppure unite da analoghe sensazioni ed emozioni. <br />È quanto attesta questo bellissimo romanzo che, a partire dal titolo, costituisce un tributo al capolavoro di Virginia Woolf, "Mrs. Dalloway" - originariamente destinato nelle intenzioni dell'autrice ad avere come titolo proprio "The Hou</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15181698944642681723">Un'opera letteraria può essere pensata, scritta, letta, amata e addirittura vissuta. <br />E può divenire, travalicando i confini dello spazio e del tempo, una sorta di collegamento tra persone molto dissimili tra loro, eppure unite da analoghe sensazioni ed emozioni. <br />È quanto attesta questo bellissimo romanzo che, a partire dal titolo, costituisce un tributo al capolavoro di Virginia Woolf, "Mrs. Dalloway" - originariamente destinato nelle intenzioni dell'autrice ad avere come titolo proprio "The Hours"- del quale riprende le situazioni, lo stile e le tematiche, proiettandoli come in un gioco di specchi nelle esistenze di tre donne vissute in epoche diverse: la stessa Virginia Woolf negli anni '20, periodo in cui sta progettando "Mrs. Dalloway"; Laura Brown, casalinga degli anni '40, che sta leggendo "Mrs. Dalloway" per evadere da un ménage coniugale opprimente; Clarissa Vaughan, editor newyorkese dei giorni nostri, soprannominata "Mrs. Dalloway" dall'amico di sempre, Richard, amore mancato di gioventù. <br /><br />Tre donne ritratte in una giornata particolare - non a caso una radiosa giornata di giugno per tutte e tre, come per Mrs. Dalloway -, in uno di quei momenti cruciali in cui si fa un bilancio della propria vita filtrandola al vaglio dei sogni e delle speranze di un tempo, nel rimpianto struggente delle opportunità perdute. <br />Il senso di fallimento sembra sommergere ogni cosa: la carriera, la vita privata, gli affetti. Non importa quale sia stato l'elemento scatenante della crisi: una torta di compleanno mal riuscita, o un mancato invito a pranzo, o semplicemente la visita dei famigliari più intimi. Ci si sente come sull'orlo di un precipizio e ciò che importa è individuare il senso di ciò che si è e di quanto si è fatto della propria vita, trovando le motivazioni per affrontare "le ore" che seguiranno. <br />Perché è in momenti come questi che il pensiero della morte esercita le sue lusinghe, presentandosi come consolatoria via di fuga, come estrema libertà di gestire il proprio destino.<br />Ed è a questo punto che interviene la letteratura, unica certezza ed unica "realtà" comprensibile e attestabile. Infatti scrivendo un libro, si può trovare sollievo dalle proprie ossessioni e dai propri demoni; con un libro tra le mani si può accantonare l'idea del suicidio e scegliere di affrontare la battaglia della vita facendo semplicemente "del proprio meglio"; e da un libro, rivivendone la vicenda portante nei tratti essenziali, si può trarre consolazione dal dolore, fiducia e gratitudine per il tempo che resta. <br />Nonostante tutto.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15181698944642681723">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1247706730?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 10, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/12490304-emanuel" class="user" title="Emanuel">Emanuel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1247706730">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15675967552462098343">Perdi a conta às vezes que vi a adaptação cinematográfica desta obra. Ao longo da leitura foi-me difícil alcançar a distância necessária para não estar a elaborar comparações. <br />No fundo é uma bonita homenagem à complexa plasticidade da emoção humana, à mulher, e a Virginia em particular. Escrita acessível e bela. Além das bases já existentes onde "As Horas" se alicerça, pouco mais me ofereceu que um distanciamento profundo, que no ecrã, até pelos simples silêncios, me devolve a aura de Virginia.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15675967552462098343">Perdi a conta às vezes que vi a adaptação cinematográfica desta obra. Ao longo da leitura foi-me difícil alcançar a distância necessária para não estar a elaborar comparações. <br />No fundo é uma bonita homenagem à complexa plasticidade da emoção humana, à mulher, e a Virginia em particular. Escrita acessível e bela. Além das bases já existentes onde "As Horas" se alicerça, pouco mais me ofereceu que um distanciamento profundo, que no ecrã, até pelos simples silêncios, me devolve a aura de Virginia. <br />Talvez não se deva comparar realizações tão distintas de uma mesma ideia, sendo isso para mim inevitável neste caso, constato que nem sempre um livro é melhor do que um posterior novo olhar sobre o seu universo.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_46570539">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/46570539" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1377744-margaret" class="left imgcol" title="Margaret"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1217389741p2/1377744.jpg" alt="Margaret" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/46570539?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 19, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1377744-margaret" class="user" title="Margaret">Margaret</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer46570539">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3124303152323343302">Well at first I made it to "sluttish widow" and then I threw the book down. I picked it up again and made it the part about a woman agreeing to be harmless so her husband will provide for her and then I just decided to skip Clarissa's entire first chapter and move on to the next chapter on Woolf. Thus far I am wondering why this book won the Pulitzer Prize . . . So I have been reading the Woolf and Brown chapters and skimming the Clarissa chapters (I absolutely loathe these; the author is so cra</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3124303152323343302">Well at first I made it to "sluttish widow" and then I threw the book down. I picked it up again and made it the part about a woman agreeing to be harmless so her husband will provide for her and then I just decided to skip Clarissa's entire first chapter and move on to the next chapter on Woolf. Thus far I am wondering why this book won the Pulitzer Prize . . . So I have been reading the Woolf and Brown chapters and skimming the Clarissa chapters (I absolutely loathe these; the author is so crass in his depiction of sexuality and the tiresome Oedipal alusions seem so dated and unoriginal) and I find I am enjoying the Woolf chapters but these chapters aren't necessarily new material since Cunningham had volumes of Woolf's writing to build upon for his story. <br /><br />I finally finished and although I did enjoy the Woolf chapters at first they quickly went downhill. It really galls me that Cunningham basically stole Woolf's novel (uses the same characters, names, etc.), badly imitates her writing style, and makes a mess of her life and wins a Pulitzer Prize for it. I'm not a Woolf fan--I can take or leave her--but she was an incredibly writer who was not recognized in her lifetime because she was a woman. Cunningham's book is less homage and more thievery.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3124303152323343302">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('YDB4YJtbyuI8FQK7sLsjxhl5eQ8MOYYknXh1mcAyRiSFe4zJaNqif4Dl+yJs1hTbdXFcf0moYp+auCvcggUP2g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('o+F20/pSkQnon9/drtWeARioQkuleDjIfn7zIRWBUJZGqoJ6CdP5lFRvJkRyuKkcdKBnO+Dp3HN5vq1kV7YZaA==')})
  }
//]]&gt;
</script>

The novel balances a vast cast of intricately enmeshed characters as they react over the course of three tense days in September 1943 to a racial incident on a U.S. Army airbase in Florida. The reader is acutely aware of the war raging abroad and the effect it has had, or will have, on the multitude of servicemen who populate Cozzens\'s immense canvas. As Noel Perrin commented in The Washington Post Book World: &amp;quot;There is material for two or three hundred movies in Guard of Honor.&amp;quot;----&amp;quot;No other American novelist of our time writes with such profound understanding of the wellsprings of human character and of the social pressures that help to form it,&amp;quot; said Orville Prescott in The New York Times. As Brendan Gill observed in The New Yorker: &amp;quot;Every page of Guard of Honor gives the impression of a writer at the very top of his powers setting out to accomplish nothing less than his masterwork.&amp;quot; The Modern Library has played a significant role in American cultural life for the better part of a century. The series was founded in 1917 by the publishers Boni and Liveright and eight years later acquired by Bennett Cerf and Donald Klopfer. It provided the foun-dation for their next publishing venture, Random House. The Modern Library has been a staple of the American book trade, providing readers with affordable hardbound editions of important works of literature and thought. For the Modern Library\'s seventy-fifth anniversary, Random House redesigned the series, restoring as its emblem the running torchbearer created by Lucian Bernhard in 1925 and refurbishing jackets, bindings, and type, as well as inaugurating a new program of selecting titles. It was the first novel by a Georgia author to win a Pulitzer, soon followed by Margaret Mitchell\'s Gone With the Wind in 1937. In fact, Lamb was largely responsible for the discovery of Gone With the Wind; after reading Miller\'s novel, Macmillan editor Harold S. Latham sought other southern novels and authors, and found Margaret Mitchell.\n\nCaroline Miller was fascinated by the other Old South—not the romantic inhabitants of Gone With the Wind, but rather the poor people of the south Georgia backwoods, who never owned a slave or planned to fight a war. The story of Cean and Lonzo, a young couple who begin their married lives two decades before the Civil War, Lamb in His Bosom is a fascinating account of social customs and material realities among settlers of the Georgia frontier. At the same time, Lamb in His Bosom transcends regional history as Miller\'s quietly lyrical prose style pays poignant tribute to a woman\'s life lived close to nature—the nature outside her and the The novel moves through a single year and, at the same time, a decade of years, from the spring arrival of the family at their mortgaged farm to the winter 10 years later, when the ravages of drought, fire, and personal anguish have led to the deaths of two of the five. Like Ethan Frome, the relatively brief, intense story evokes the torment possible among people isolated and driven by strong feelings of love and hate that, unexpressed, lead inevitably to doom. Reviewers in the thirties praised the novel, calling its prose &amp;quot;profoundly moving music,&amp;quot; expressing incredulity &amp;quot;that this mature style and this mature point of view are those of a young women in her twenties,&amp;quot; comparing the book to &amp;quot;the luminous work of Willa Cather,&amp;quot; and, with prescience, suggesting that it &amp;quot;has that rare quality     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1432.Michael_Cunningham">About Michael Cunningham</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/1432.Michael_Cunningham"><img alt="1432" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1398891471p2/1432.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer13349371454479688736">Michael Cunningham is the author of the novels A Home at the End of the World, Flesh and Blood, The Hours (winner of the Pen/Faulkner Award &amp; Pulitzer Prize), Specimen Days, and By Nightfall, as well as the non-fiction book, Land's End: A Walk in Provincetown. His new novel, The Snow Queen, will be published in May of 2014. He lives in New York, and teaches at Yale University.</span>

    </div>
    <a href="/author/show/1432.Michael_Cunningham" class="actionLink moreLink">More about Michael Cunningham...</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/2245431-the-hours">Quotes from The Hours: A Novel</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“We throw our parties; we abandon our families to live alone in Canada; we struggle to write books that do not change the world, despite our gifts and our unstinting efforts, our most extravagant hopes. We live our lives, do whatever we do, and then we sleep. It's as simple and ordinary as that. A few jump out windows, or drown themselves, or take pills; more die by accident; and most of us are slowly devoured by some disease, or, if we're very fortunate, by time itself. There's just this for consolation: an hour here or there when our lives seem, against all odds and expectations, to burst open and give us everything we've ever imagined, though everyone but children (and perhaps even they) know these hours will inevitably be followed by others, far darker and more difficult. Still, we cherish the city, the morning; we hope, more than anything, for more. Heaven only knows why we love it so...”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/95737-we-throw-our-parties-we-abandon-our-families-to-live" class="actionLinkLite">323 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“Dear Leonard. To look life in the face. Always to look life in the face and to know it for what it is. At last to know it. To love it for what it is, and then, to put it away. Leonard. Always the years between us. Always the years. Always the love. Always the hours.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/47211-dear-leonard-to-look-life-in-the-face-always-to" class="actionLinkLite">318 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a href="/work/quotes/2245431-the-hours" style="float: right" class="actionLink">More quotes…</a>
